<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Great Barrier Reef</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Coral reef system located in the Coral Sea in Australia</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef_(disambiguation)" title="Great Barrier Reef (disambiguation)">Great Barrier Reef (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

protected area</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Great Barrier Reef</b> is the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reef</a> system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRWHV_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBRWHV-2">[2]</a></sup> composed of over 2,900 individual reefs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and 900 islands stretching for over 2,300 kilometres (1,400 mi) over an area of approximately 344,400 square kilometres (133,000 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1975review_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-1975review-5">[5]</a></sup> The reef is located in the <a href="/wiki/Coral_Sea" title="Coral Sea">Coral Sea</a>, off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, separated from the coast by a channel 100 miles wide in places and over 200 feet deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The Great Barrier Reef can be seen from <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> and is the world's biggest single structure made by living organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> This reef structure is composed of and built by billions of tiny organisms, known as <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">coral</a> <a href="/wiki/Polyp_(zoology)" title="Polyp (zoology)">polyps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billions_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-billions-8">[8]</a></sup> It supports a wide diversity of life and was selected as a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRWHV_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-GBRWHV-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a> labelled it one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_World#Seven_Natural_Wonders_of_the_World" title="Seven Wonders of the World">seven natural wonders of the world</a> in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Australian World Heritage places included it in its list in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The Queensland National Trust named it a state icon of Queensland in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>A large part of the reef is protected by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef_Marine_Park" title="Great Barrier Reef Marine Park">Great Barrier Reef Marine Park</a>, which helps to limit the impact of human use, such as fishing and tourism. Other <a href="/wiki/Environmental_threats_to_the_Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Environmental threats to the Great Barrier Reef">environmental pressures on the reef</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> include <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> accompanied by mass <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">coral bleaching</a>, dumping of dredging sludge and cyclic population outbreaks of the <a href="/wiki/Crown-of-thorns_starfish" title="Crown-of-thorns starfish">crown-of-thorns starfish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> According to a study published in October 2012 by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences">Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences</a></i>, the reef has lost more than half its coral cover since 1985, a finding reaffirmed by a 2020 study which found over half of the reef's coral cover to have been lost between 1995 and 2017, with the effects of a widespread 2020 bleaching event not yet quantified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Great Barrier Reef has long been known to and used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Aborigines" title="Australian Aborigines">Aboriginal Australian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torres_Strait_Islander" title="Torres Strait Islander">Torres Strait Islander</a> peoples, and is an important part of local groups' cultures and spirituality. The reef is a very popular destination for tourists, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Whitsunday_Islands" title="Whitsunday Islands">Whitsunday Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cairns" title="Cairns">Cairns</a> regions. Tourism is an important economic activity for the region, generating over <a href="/wiki/Australian_dollar" title="Australian dollar">AUD$</a>3 billion per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economics_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-economics-15">[15]</a></sup> In November 2014, Google launched <a href="/wiki/Google_Maps#Street_View" title="Google Maps">Google Underwater Street View</a> in 3D of the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>A March 2016 report stated that <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">coral bleaching</a> was more widespread than previously thought, seriously affecting the northern parts of the reef as a result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming_on_oceans" title="Effects of global warming on oceans">warming ocean temperatures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In October 2016, <i><a href="/wiki/Outside_(magazine)" title="Outside (magazine)">Outside</a></i> published an obituary for the reef;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> the article was criticized for being premature and hindering efforts to bolster the resilience of the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In March 2017, the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> published a paper showing that huge sections of an 800-kilometre (500 mi) stretch in the northern part of the reef had died in the course of 2016 due to high water temperatures, an event that the authors put down to the effects of global climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The percentage of baby corals being born on the Great Barrier Reef dropped drastically in 2018 and scientists are describing it as the early stage of a "huge natural selection event unfolding". Many of the mature breeding adults died in the bleaching events of 2016–17 leading to low coral birth rates. The types of corals that reproduced also changed, leading to a "long-term reorganisation of the reef ecosystem if the trend continues."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Act 1975 (section 54) stipulates an Outlook Report on the Reef's health, pressures, and future every five years. The last report was published in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In March 2022, another mass bleaching event has been confirmed, which raised further concerns about the future of this reef system, especially when considering the possible effects of <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> weather phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology_and_geography">Geology and geography</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Part_of_Great_Barrier_Reef_from_Helicopter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1232" data-file-width="1632" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Part_of_Great_Barrier_Reef_from_Helicopter.jpg/220px-Part_of_Great_Barrier_Reef_from_Helicopter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Part_of_Great_Barrier_Reef_from_Helicopter.jpg/330px-Part_of_Great_Barrier_Reef_from_Helicopter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Part_of_Great_Barrier_Reef_from_Helicopter.jpg/440px-Part_of_Great_Barrier_Reef_from_Helicopter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Part_of_Great_Barrier_Reef_from_Helicopter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial photography</div></div></div>
<p>The Great Barrier Reef is a distinct feature of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Australian_Cordillera" title="East Australian Cordillera">East Australian Cordillera</a> division. It reaches from <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait" title="Torres Strait">Torres Strait</a> (between <a href="/wiki/Bramble_Cay" title="Bramble Cay">Bramble Cay</a>, its northernmost island, and the south coast of <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>) in the north to the unnamed passage between <a href="/wiki/Lady_Elliot_Island" title="Lady Elliot Island">Lady Elliot Island</a> (its southernmost island) and <a href="/wiki/Fraser_Island" title="Fraser Island">Fraser Island</a> in the south. Lady Elliot Island is located 1,915 km (1,190 mi) southeast of Bramble Cay <a href="/wiki/As_the_crow_flies" title="As the crow flies">as the crow flies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-map_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-map-24">[24]</a></sup> It includes the smaller <a href="/wiki/Murray_Island,_Queensland" title="Murray Island, Queensland">Murray Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plate_tectonic" title="Plate tectonic">plate tectonic</a> theory indicates Australia has <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">moved northwards</a> at a rate of 7 cm (2.8 in) per year, starting during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cainozoic" title="Cainozoic">Cenozoic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18">: 18 </span></sup> Eastern Australia experienced a period of <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_uplift" title="Tectonic uplift">tectonic uplift</a>, which moved the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_divide" title="Drainage divide">drainage divide</a> in Queensland 400 km (250 mi) inland. Also during this time, Queensland experienced volcanic eruptions leading to central and shield <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> flows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 19">: 19 </span></sup> Some of these became <a href="/wiki/High_island" title="High island">high islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 26">: 26 </span></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Coral_Sea" title="Coral Sea">Coral Sea</a> <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_basin" title="Oceanic basin">Basin</a> formed, coral reefs began to grow in the Basin, but until about 25 million years ago, northern Queensland was still in temperate waters south of the tropics—too cool to support coral growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 27">: 27 </span></sup> The Great Barrier Reef's development history is complex; after Queensland drifted into tropical waters, it was largely influenced by reef growth and decline as sea level changed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 27–28">: 27–28 </span></sup>
</p><p>Reefs can increase in diameter by 1 to 3 centimetres (0.39 to 1.18 in) per year, and grow vertically anywhere from 1 to 25 cm (0.39 to 9.84 in) per year; however, they grow only above a depth of 150 metres (490 ft) due to their need for sunlight, and cannot grow above sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> When Queensland edged into tropical waters 24 million years ago, some coral grew,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 29">: 29 </span></sup> but a <a href="/wiki/Sedimentation" title="Sedimentation">sedimentation</a> <a href="/wiki/Regime" title="Regime">regime</a> quickly developed with erosion of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Dividing_Range" title="Great Dividing Range">Great Dividing Range</a>; creating <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">river deltas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelagic_sediments" title="Pelagic sediments">oozes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turbidite" title="Turbidite">turbidites</a>, unsuitable conditions for coral growth. 10 million years ago, the sea level significantly lowered, which further enabled sedimentation. The reef's substrate may have needed to build up from the sediment until its edge was too far away for suspended sediments to inhibit coral growth. In addition, approximately 400,000 years ago there was a particularly warm <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">Interglacial</a> period with higher sea levels and a 4 °C (7 °F) water temperature change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 37">: 37 </span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heron_Island.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="681" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Heron_Island.jpg/220px-Heron_Island.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Heron_Island.jpg/330px-Heron_Island.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Heron_Island.jpg/440px-Heron_Island.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heron_Island.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Heron_Island_(Queensland)" title="Heron Island (Queensland)">Heron Island</a>, a coral cay in the southern Great Barrier Reef</div></div></div>
<p>The land that formed the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/substrate" title="wikt:substrate">substrate</a> of the current Great Barrier Reef was a <a href="/wiki/Coastal_plain" title="Coastal plain">coastal plain</a> formed from the eroded sediments of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Dividing_Range" title="Great Dividing Range">Great Dividing Range</a> with some larger hills (most of which were themselves remnants of older reefs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIMSage_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIMSage-29">[29]</a></sup> or, in rare cases, volcanoes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 26">: 26 </span></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18">: 18 </span></sup>
The Reef Research Centre, a <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_Research_Centre" title="Cooperative Research Centre">Cooperative Research Centre</a>, has found coral 'skeleton' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposit_(geology)" title="Deposit (geology)">deposits</a> that date back half a million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCage_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRCage-30">[30]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef_Marine_Park_Authority" title="Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority">Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority</a> (GBRMPA) considers the earliest evidence of complete reef structures to have been 600,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRMPAage_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBRMPAage-31">[31]</a></sup> According to the GBRMPA, the current, living reef structure is believed to have begun growing on the older platform about 20,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRMPAage_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-GBRMPAage-31">[31]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Australian_Institute_of_Marine_Science" title="Australian Institute of Marine Science">Australian Institute of Marine Science</a> agrees, placing the beginning of the growth of the current reef at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>. At around that time, sea level was 120 metres (390 ft) lower than it is today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIMSage_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-AIMSage-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arlington-Reef-2018-aerial-view-Luka-Peternel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Arlington-Reef-2018-aerial-view-Luka-Peternel.jpg/220px-Arlington-Reef-2018-aerial-view-Luka-Peternel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Arlington-Reef-2018-aerial-view-Luka-Peternel.jpg/330px-Arlington-Reef-2018-aerial-view-Luka-Peternel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Arlington-Reef-2018-aerial-view-Luka-Peternel.jpg/440px-Arlington-Reef-2018-aerial-view-Luka-Peternel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arlington-Reef-2018-aerial-view-Luka-Peternel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of <a href="/wiki/Arlington_Reef" title="Arlington Reef">Arlington Reef</a></div></div></div>
<p>From 20,000 years ago until 6,000 years ago, <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rose</a> steadily around the world. As it rose, the corals could then grow higher on the newly submerged maritime margins of the hills of the coastal plain. By around 13,000 years ago the sea level was only 60 metres (200 ft) lower than the present day, and corals began to surround the hills of the coastal plain, which were, by then, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_islands" title="Continental islands">continental islands</a>. As the sea level rose further still, most of the continental islands were submerged. The corals could then overgrow the submerged hills, to form the present <a href="/wiki/Cay" title="Cay">cays</a> and reefs. Sea level here has not risen significantly in the last 6,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIMSage_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-AIMSage-29">[29]</a></sup> The CRC Reef Research Centre estimates the age of the present, living reef structure at 6,000 to 8,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCage_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRCage-30">[30]</a></sup> The shallow water reefs that can be seen in air-photographs and satellite images cover an area of 20,679 km<sup>2</sup>, most (about 80%) of which<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> has grown on top of limestone platforms that are relics of past (Pleistocene) phases of reef growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-9"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The remains of an ancient barrier reef similar to the Great Barrier Reef can be found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Kimberley" title="The Kimberley">The Kimberley</a>, Western Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Great Barrier Reef World Heritage Area has been divided into 70 <a href="/wiki/Bioregion" title="Bioregion">bioregions</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> of which 30 are reef bioregions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In the northern part of the Great Barrier Reef, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ribbon_reef" title="Ribbon reef">ribbon reefs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deltaic_reef" title="Deltaic reef">deltaic reefs</a> have formed; these structures are not found in the rest of the reef system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRCage_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRCage-30">[30]</a></sup> A previously undiscovered reef, 500 meters tall and 1.5 km wide at the base, was found in the northern area in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> There are no <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atolls</a> in the system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-10"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">: 7 </span></sup> and reefs attached to the mainland are rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-11"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18">: 18 </span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fringing_reef" title="Fringing reef">Fringing reefs</a> are distributed widely, but are most common towards the southern part of the Great Barrier Reef, attached to high islands, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Whitsunday_Islands" title="Whitsunday Islands">Whitsunday Islands</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lagoonal_reef" title="Lagoonal reef">Lagoonal reefs</a> are found in the southern Great Barrier Reef, and further north, off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Princess_Charlotte_Bay" title="Princess Charlotte Bay">Princess Charlotte Bay</a>. <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/crescentic" title="wikt:crescentic">Crescentic</a> reefs are the most common shape of reef in the middle of the system, for example the reefs surrounding <a href="/wiki/Lizard_Island" title="Lizard Island">Lizard Island</a>. Crescentic reefs are also found in the far north of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park, and in the Swain Reefs (<a href="/wiki/20th_parallel_south" title="20th parallel south">20</a>–<a href="/wiki/22nd_parallel_south" title="22nd parallel south">22 degrees south</a>). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planar_reef" title="Planar reef">Planar reefs</a> are found in the northern and southern parts, near <a href="/wiki/Cape_York_Peninsula" title="Cape York Peninsula">Cape York Peninsula</a>, Princess Charlotte Bay, and Cairns. Most of the islands on the reef are found on planar reefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-12"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 158–160">: 158–160 </span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wonky_hole" title="Wonky hole">Wonky holes</a> can have localised impact on the reef, providing upwellings of fresh water, sometimes rich in nutrients contributing to <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Threatened_species_known_to_occur_in_the_Great_Barrier_Reef_World_Heritage_Area" title="Threatened species known to occur in the Great Barrier Reef World Heritage Area">Threatened species known to occur in the Great Barrier Reef World Heritage Area</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg/220px-Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg/330px-Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg/440px-Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coral_Outcrop_Flynn_Reef.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A variety of colourful corals on Flynn Reef near Cairns</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moore_Reef_underwater_ReefScape.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3056" data-file-width="4592" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Moore_Reef_underwater_ReefScape.jpg/220px-Moore_Reef_underwater_ReefScape.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Moore_Reef_underwater_ReefScape.jpg/330px-Moore_Reef_underwater_ReefScape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Moore_Reef_underwater_ReefScape.jpg/440px-Moore_Reef_underwater_ReefScape.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moore_Reef_underwater_ReefScape.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Moore Reef</div></div></div>
<p>The Great Barrier Reef supports an extraordinary diversity of life, including many <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">vulnerable</a> or <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a>, some of which may be <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> to the reef system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRMPA_Fauna_and_Flora_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBRMPA_Fauna_and_Flora-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turtle06.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="972" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Turtle06.jpg/170px-Turtle06.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Turtle06.jpg/255px-Turtle06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Turtle06.jpg/340px-Turtle06.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turtle06.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Green_sea_turtle" title="Green sea turtle">green sea turtle</a> on the Great Barrier Reef</div></div></div>
<p>Thirty species of <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">cetaceans</a> have been recorded in the Great Barrier Reef, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwarf_minke_whale" title="Dwarf minke whale">dwarf minke whale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific_humpback_dolphin" title="Indo-Pacific humpback dolphin">Indo-Pacific humpback dolphin</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Humpback_whale" title="Humpback whale">humpback whale</a>. Large populations of <a href="/wiki/Dugong" title="Dugong">dugongs</a> live there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRMPA_Fauna_and_Flora_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-GBRMPA_Fauna_and_Flora-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_species_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_species-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> More than 1,500 <a href="/wiki/List_of_fishes_of_the_Coral_Sea" title="List of fishes of the Coral Sea">fish species</a> live on the reef, including the <a href="/wiki/Clownfish" title="Clownfish">clownfish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two-spot_red_snapper" title="Two-spot red snapper">red bass</a>, red-throat emperor, and several species of <a href="/wiki/Lutjanidae" title="Lutjanidae">snapper</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coral_trout" title="Coral trout">coral trout</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_species_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_species-42">[42]</a></sup> Forty-nine species <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_synchrony" title="Reproductive synchrony">mass spawn</a>, while eighty-four other species spawn elsewhere in their range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Seventeen species of <a href="/wiki/Sea_snake" title="Sea snake">sea snake</a> live on the Great Barrier Reef in warm waters up to 50 metres (160 ft) deep and are more common in the southern than in the northern section. None found in the Great Barrier Reef World Heritage Area are endemic, nor are any endangered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flora_and_Fauna_2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flora_and_Fauna_2-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Six species of <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a> come to the reef to breed: the <a href="/wiki/Green_sea_turtle" title="Green sea turtle">green sea turtle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leatherback_sea_turtle" title="Leatherback sea turtle">leatherback sea turtle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawksbill_turtle" title="Hawksbill turtle">hawksbill turtle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loggerhead_sea_turtle" title="Loggerhead sea turtle">loggerhead sea turtle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatback_turtle" title="Flatback turtle">flatback turtle</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olive_ridley" title="Olive ridley">olive ridley</a>. The green sea turtles on the Great Barrier Reef have two <a href="/wiki/Population_genetics" title="Population genetics">genetically distinct populations</a>, one in the northern part of the reef and the other in the southern part.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rap_turtle_dugong_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-rap_turtle_dugong-46">[46]</a></sup> Fifteen species of <a href="/wiki/Seagrass" title="Seagrass">seagrass</a> in beds attract the dugongs and turtles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_species_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_species-42">[42]</a></sup> and provide fish habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-13"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 133">: 133 </span></sup> The most common <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> of seagrasses are <i><a href="/wiki/Halophila" title="Halophila">Halophila</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Halodule" title="Halodule">Halodule</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile" title="Saltwater crocodile">Saltwater crocodiles</a> live in <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangrove</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salt_marsh" title="Salt marsh">salt marshes</a> on the coast near the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Nesting has not been reported, and the salt water crocodile population in the GBRWHA is wide-ranging but low density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flora_and_Fauna_2_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flora_and_Fauna_2-45">[45]</a></sup> Around 125 species of <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">shark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stingray" title="Stingray">stingray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skate_(fish)" title="Skate (fish)">skates</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chimaera" title="Chimaera">chimaera</a> live on the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flora_and_Fauna_4_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flora_and_Fauna_4-50">[50]</a></sup> Close to 5,000 species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">mollusc</a> have been recorded on the reef, including the <a href="/wiki/Giant_clam" title="Giant clam">giant clam</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Nudibranch" title="Nudibranch">nudibranchs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cone_snail" title="Cone snail">cone snails</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_species_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_species-42">[42]</a></sup> Forty-nine species of <a href="/wiki/Pipefish" title="Pipefish">pipefish</a> and nine species of <a href="/wiki/Seahorse" title="Seahorse">seahorse</a> have been recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flora_and_Fauna_2_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Flora_and_Fauna_2-45">[45]</a></sup> At least seven species of frog inhabit the islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flora_and_Fauna_5_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flora_and_Fauna_5-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>215 species of birds (including 22 species of seabirds and 32 species of shorebirds) visit the reef or nest or roost on the islands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-14"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 450–451">: 450–451 </span></sup> including the <a href="/wiki/White-bellied_sea_eagle" title="White-bellied sea eagle">white-bellied sea eagle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roseate_tern" title="Roseate tern">roseate tern</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_species_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_species-42">[42]</a></sup> Most nesting sites are on islands in the northern and southern regions of the Great Barrier Reef, with 1.4 to 1.7 million birds using the sites to breed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The islands of the Great Barrier Reef also support 2,195 known plant species; three of these are endemic. The northern islands have 300–350 plant species which tend to be woody, whereas the southern islands have 200 which tend to be herbaceous; the Whitsunday region is the most diverse, supporting 1,141 species. The plants are propagated by birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flora_and_Fauna_5_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flora_and_Fauna_5-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acanthurus_lineatus_Flynn_Reef.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1259" data-file-width="2093" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Acanthurus_lineatus_Flynn_Reef.jpg/220px-Acanthurus_lineatus_Flynn_Reef.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Acanthurus_lineatus_Flynn_Reef.jpg/330px-Acanthurus_lineatus_Flynn_Reef.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Acanthurus_lineatus_Flynn_Reef.jpg/440px-Acanthurus_lineatus_Flynn_Reef.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acanthurus_lineatus_Flynn_Reef.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Acanthurus_lineatus" title="Acanthurus lineatus">striped surgeonfish</a> amongst the coral on Flynn Reef</div></div></div>
<p>There are at least 330 species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascidian" title="Ascidian">ascidians</a> on the reef system with the diameter of 1–10 cm (0.4–4 in). Between 300 and 500 species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bryozoan" title="Bryozoan">bryozoans</a> live on the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flora_and_Fauna_4_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flora_and_Fauna_4-50">[50]</a></sup> Four hundred coral species, both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_coral" title="Hard coral">hard corals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_coral" title="Soft coral">soft corals</a> inhabit the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_species_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_species-42">[42]</a></sup> The majority of these spawn <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a>, breeding in mass spawning events that are triggered by the rising sea temperatures of spring and summer, the lunar cycle, and the diurnal cycle. Reefs in the inner Great Barrier Reef spawn during the week after the full moon in October, while the outer reefs spawn in November and December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Its common soft corals belong to 36 genera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Five hundred species of marine algae or <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a> live on the reef,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_species_42-6"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_species-42">[42]</a></sup> including thirteen species of genus <i><a href="/wiki/Halimeda" title="Halimeda">Halimeda</a></i>, which deposit <a href="/wiki/Calcareous" title="Calcareous">calcareous</a> mounds up to 100 metres (110 yd) wide, creating mini-ecosystems on their surface which have been compared to <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a> cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-15"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 185">: 185 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_threats">Environmental threats</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_threats_to_the_Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Environmental threats to the Great Barrier Reef">Environmental threats to the Great Barrier Reef</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GBReef_TempChlorophyll_200602.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="779" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/GBReef_TempChlorophyll_200602.jpg/220px-GBReef_TempChlorophyll_200602.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/GBReef_TempChlorophyll_200602.jpg/330px-GBReef_TempChlorophyll_200602.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/GBReef_TempChlorophyll_200602.jpg/440px-GBReef_TempChlorophyll_200602.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GBReef_TempChlorophyll_200602.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sea temperature and <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">bleaching</a> of the Great Barrier Reef</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a>, pollution, <a href="/wiki/Crown-of-thorns_starfish" title="Crown-of-thorns starfish">crown-of-thorns starfish</a> and fishing are the primary threats to the health of this reef system. Other threats include shipping accidents, <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a>, and tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC46_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC46-56">[56]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeletal_Eroding_Band" title="Skeletal Eroding Band">Skeletal Eroding Band</a>, a disease of bony corals caused by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protozoan" title="Protozoan">protozoan</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Halofolliculina_corallasia" title="Halofolliculina corallasia">Halofolliculina corallasia</a></i>, affects 31 coral species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> According to a 2012 study by the <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a>, since 1985, the Great Barrier Reef has lost more than half of its corals with two-thirds of the loss occurring from 1998 due to the factors listed before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span></h3>
<p>The Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority considers the greatest threat to the Great Barrier Reef to be climate change, causing ocean warming which increases <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">coral bleaching</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Mass coral bleaching events due to <a href="/wiki/Marine_heatwave" title="Marine heatwave">marine heatwaves</a> occurred in the summers of 1998, 2002, 2006, 2016, 2017 and 2020,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-62">[62]</a></sup> and coral bleaching is expected to become an annual occurrence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tele_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-tele-63">[63]</a></sup> In 2020, a study found that the Great Barrier Reef has lost more than half of its corals since 1995 due to warmer seas driven by climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-62">[62]</a></sup> As global warming continues, corals will not be able to keep up with increasing ocean temperatures. Coral bleaching events lead to increased disease susceptibility, which causes detrimental ecological effects for reef communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Littman_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Littman-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2017 <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> published in a draft decision, expressing serious concern about the impact of coral bleaching on the Great Barrier Reef.  The draft decision also warned Australia that it will not meet the targets of the Reef 2050 report without considerable work to improve water quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Climate change has implications for other forms of reef life—some fish's preferred temperature range leads them to seek new habitat, thus increasing chick mortality in predatory seabirds. Climate change will also affect the sea turtle's population and available habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRMPAclimatechange_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBRMPAclimatechange-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bleaching events in benthic coral communities (deeper than 20 metres or 66 feet) in the Great Barrier reef are not as well documented as those at shallower depths, but recent research has shown that benthic communities are just as negatively impacted in the face of rising ocean temperatures. Five Great Barrier Reef species of large benthic corals were found bleached under elevated temperatures, affirming that benthic corals are vulnerable to thermal stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmidt_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span></h3>
<p>Another key threat faced by the Great Barrier Reef is <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">pollution</a> and declining <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water quality</a>. The rivers of north-eastern Australia pollute the Reef during tropical flood events. Over 90% of this pollution comes from farm <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> 80% of the land adjacent to the Great Barrier Reef is used for farming including intensive cropping of sugar cane, and major beef cattle grazing. Farming practices damage the reef due to <a href="/wiki/Overgrazing" title="Overgrazing">overgrazing</a>, increased run-off of agricultural sediments, nutrients and chemicals including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertiliser" title="Fertiliser">fertilisers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> representing a major health risk for the coral and biodiversity of the reefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a 2016 report, while higher regulation contributes to less overall pollution from "other land uses, such as industrial, mining, port development, dredging and urban development", these can still be locally significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Sediments containing high levels of copper and other heavy metals sourced from the <a href="/wiki/Ok_Tedi_Mine" title="Ok Tedi Mine">Ok Tedi Mine</a> in Papua New Guinea are a potential pollution risk for the far northern Great Barrier Reef and <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait" title="Torres Strait">Torres Strait</a> regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some 67% of corals died in the reef's worst-hit northern section, the ARC Centre of Excellence for Coral Reef Studies report said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_coastal_wetland">Loss of coastal wetland</span></h4>
<p>The runoff problem is exacerbated by the loss of coastal <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> which act as a natural filter for toxins and help deposit sediment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wetlands_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-wetlands-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brodie07_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brodie07-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> It is thought that the poor water quality is due to increased light and oxygen <a href="/wiki/Competition_(biology)" title="Competition (biology)">competition</a> from <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waterquality_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-waterquality-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eutrophication">Eutrophication</span></h4>
<p>Farming fertiliser runoff release <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> into the oceanic ecosystem, and these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limiting_nutrient" title="Limiting nutrient">limiting nutrients</a> cause massive algal growth which eventually leads to a reduction in oxygen available for other creatures in a process called <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a>. This decreases the biodiversity in the affected areas, altering the species composition. A study by Katharina Fabricius and Glen Death of <a href="/wiki/Australian_Institute_of_Marine_Science" title="Australian Institute of Marine Science">Australian Institute of Marine Science</a> found that hard corals numbers were almost double on reefs that were far from agricultural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fertilizers also increase the amount of phytoplankton available for the crown-of-thorns starfish larvae to consume. A study showed that a doubling of the chlorophyll in the water leads to a tenfold increase in the crown-of-thorns starfish larvae's survival rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sediment_runoff">Sediment runoff</span></h4>
<p>Sediment runoff from farming carries chemicals into the reef environment also reduces the amount of light available to the corals decreasing their ability to extract energy from their environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pesticides">Pesticides</span></h4>
<p>Pesticides used in farming are made up of heavy metals such as lead, mercury, arsenic and other toxins are released into the wider environment due to erosion of farm soil, which has a detrimental effect on the coral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef_69-4"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Impact_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution_from_mining">Pollution from mining</span></h4>
<p>Mining company Queensland Nickel discharged nitrate-laden water into the Great Barrier Reef in 2009 and 2011 – on the later occasion releasing 516 tonnes (508 long tons; 569 short tons) of waste water. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef_Marine_Park_Authority" title="Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority">Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority</a> (GBRMPA) stated "We have strongly encouraged the company to investigate options that do not entail releasing the material to the environment and to develop a management plan to eliminate this potential hazard; however, GBRMPA does not have legislative control over how the Yabulu tailings dam is managed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clive_Palmer's_nickel_refinery_pumped_toxic_waste_into_Great_Barrier_Reef_park_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clive_Palmer's_nickel_refinery_pumped_toxic_waste_into_Great_Barrier_Reef_park-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crown_of_thorns">Crown of thorns</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CrownofThornsStarfish_Fiji_2005-10-12.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/CrownofThornsStarfish_Fiji_2005-10-12.jpg/170px-CrownofThornsStarfish_Fiji_2005-10-12.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/CrownofThornsStarfish_Fiji_2005-10-12.jpg/255px-CrownofThornsStarfish_Fiji_2005-10-12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/CrownofThornsStarfish_Fiji_2005-10-12.jpg/340px-CrownofThornsStarfish_Fiji_2005-10-12.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CrownofThornsStarfish_Fiji_2005-10-12.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Crown-of-thorns_starfish" title="Crown-of-thorns starfish">Crown-of-thorns starfish</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Crown-of-thorns_starfish" title="Crown-of-thorns starfish">crown-of-thorns starfish</a> preys on coral polyps. Large outbreaks of these starfish can devastate reefs. In 2000, an outbreak contributed to a loss of 66% of live coral cover on sampled reefs in a study by the Reef Research Centre (RRC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cots_1999-2000_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-cots_1999-2000-78">[78]</a></sup> Outbreaks are believed to occur in natural cycles, worsened by poor water quality and overfishing of the starfish's predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cots_1999-2000_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-cots_1999-2000-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overfishing">Overfishing</span></h3>
<p>The unsustainable <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">keystone species</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triton_(mollusk)" title="Triton (mollusk)">giant Triton</a>, can disrupt <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chains</a> vital to reef life. Fishing also impacts the reef through increased water pollution from boats, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/By-catch" title="By-catch">by-catch</a> of unwanted species (such as dolphins and turtles) and <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a> from <a href="/wiki/Trawling" title="Trawling">trawling</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchors" title="Anchors">anchors</a> and nets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> As of the middle of 2004, approximately one-third of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef_Marine_Park" title="Great Barrier Reef Marine Park">Great Barrier Reef Marine Park</a> is protected from species removal of any kind, including fishing, without written permission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_barrier_oil_spill_march_2010.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Great_barrier_oil_spill_march_2010.JPG/220px-Great_barrier_oil_spill_march_2010.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Great_barrier_oil_spill_march_2010.JPG/330px-Great_barrier_oil_spill_march_2010.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Great_barrier_oil_spill_march_2010.JPG/440px-Great_barrier_oil_spill_march_2010.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_barrier_oil_spill_march_2010.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shen_Neng_1" title="Shen Neng 1">Shen Neng 1</a></i> aground on the Great Barrier Reef, 5 April 2010</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipping">Shipping</span></h3>
<p>Shipping accidents are a pressing concern, as several commercial shipping routes pass through the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBRMPAshipping_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBRMPAshipping-82">[82]</a></sup>
Although the route through the Great Barrier Reef is not easy, reef pilots consider it safer than outside the reef in the event of mechanical failure, since a ship can sit safely while being repaired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reefdoco_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-reefdoco-83">[83]</a></sup> There have been over 1,600 known shipwrecks in the Great Barrier Reef region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
On 3 April 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Bulk_carrier" title="Bulk carrier">bulk coal carrier</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shen_Neng_1" title="Shen Neng 1"><i>Shen Neng 1</i></a> ran aground on Douglas Shoals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/2010_Great_Barrier_Reef_oil_spill" title="2010 Great Barrier Reef oil spill">spilling up to four tonnes of oil</a> into the water and causing extensive damage to the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shark_culling">Shark culling</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shark_culling" title="Shark culling">Shark culling</a></div>
<p>The government of <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a> has a "shark control" program (<a href="/wiki/Shark_culling" title="Shark culling">shark culling</a>) that deliberately kills sharks throughout Queensland, including in the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shark_culling_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-shark_culling-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntd_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-ntd-88">[88]</a></sup> Environmentalists and scientists say that this program harms the <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">marine ecosystem</a>; they also say it is "outdated, cruel and ineffective".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntd_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-ntd-88">[88]</a></sup> The Queensland "shark control" program uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shark_nets" title="Shark nets">shark nets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drum_line_(shark_control)" title="Drum line (shark control)">drum lines with baited hooks</a> to kill sharks in the Great Barrier Reef – there are 173 lethal drum lines in the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shark_culling_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-shark_culling-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntd_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-ntd-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maritime_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-maritime-89">[89]</a></sup> In Queensland, sharks found alive on the baited hooks are shot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Queensland's "shark control" program killed about 50,000 sharks from 1962 to 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Also, Queensland's "shark control" program has also killed many other animals (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dolphins" title="Dolphins">dolphins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turtles" title="Turtles">turtles</a>) — the program killed 84,000 marine animals from 1962 to 2015, including in the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In 2018, <a href="/wiki/Humane_Society_International" title="Humane Society International">Humane Society International</a> filed a lawsuit against the government of Queensland to stop shark culling in the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maritime_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-maritime-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Protection_and_preservation:_Reef_2050_plan">Protection and preservation: Reef 2050 plan</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Natural_Heritage_Trust" title="Natural Heritage Trust">Natural Heritage Trust</a></div>
<p>In March 2015, the Australian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_Queensland" title="Government of Queensland">Queensland's governments</a> formed a plan for the protection and preservation of the reef's universal heritage until 2050. This 35 years plan, titled "Reef 2050 Plan" is a document proposing possible measures for the long-term management of the pollution, climate change and other issues that threaten the life span and value of this global heritage. The plan contains all the elements for measurement and improvements, including; long-term sustainability plan, water quality improvement plan and the investment plan for the protection and preservation of The Reef until 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, whereas the 2050 plan aims to incorporate protective measures such as improving water quality, reef restoration, killing of predatory starfish, it does not incorporate additional measures to address what may be the root cause the problem – climate change, which is caused by greenhouse gas emissions. As such, experts doubted whether it would be enough to save the fragile environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Another issue is that the time left to the 1.5 °C warming threshold (the temperature limit that coral reefs can still cope with<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>) is very limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of the Reef 2050 plan, an AUD$443 million grant was given to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef_Foundation" title="Great Barrier Reef Foundation">Great Barrier Reef Foundation</a> in 2018. The announcement of the grant was subject to backlash as the grant had avoided proper tender and transparency processes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_Policy:_Reef_2050_Water_Quality_Improvement_Plan">Conservation Policy: Reef 2050 Water Quality Improvement Plan</span></h3>
<p>The Great Barrier Reef contributes to the overall wellbeing of the marine biome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>  Numerous species of aquatic plants, fish and megafauna utilize the reef for feeding, shelter and mating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>  Threats such as <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>, pollution runoff and outbreaks of destructive species like the <a href="/wiki/Crown-of-thorns_starfish" title="Crown-of-thorns starfish">crown-of-thorns starfish</a> have brought about the decline of this ecosystem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> These threats to the reef are not only a danger to the organisms inhabiting it, but also the economy of this region, a large part of which relies on revenue from ecotourism of the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Australian_Government" title="Australian Government">Australian government</a> has had the goal of protecting this World Heritage Site since 1972 when they created The Australian Institute of Marine Science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-102">[102]</a></sup> The Australian and Queensland governments have contributed about $142.5 to their National Environmental Science Program which is how they've collected much of their data regarding threats to the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-102">[102]</a></sup> In addition, the Reef 2050 Water Quality Improvement Plan was announced in 2018 in order to help transition local communities, agricultural organizations and industries to more sustainable practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-103">[103]</a></sup>  This plan will join the Queensland government and the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority to manage the amounts of runoff that reach the Great Barrier Reef as well as mitigating crown-of-thorns starfish population flare-ups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_use">Human use</span></h2>
<p>The Great Barrier Reef has long been known to and used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aboriginal_Australian" title="Aboriginal Australian">Aboriginal Australian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torres_Strait_Islander" title="Torres Strait Islander">Torres Strait Islander</a> peoples. Aboriginal Australians have been living in the area for at least 40,000 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and Torres Strait Islanders since about 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> For these 70 or so <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clan_group" title="Clan group">clan groups</a>, the reef is also an important cultural feature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-traditionalfishing_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-traditionalfishing-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1768 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_de_Bougainville" title="Louis de Bougainville">Louis de Bougainville</a> found the reef during an exploratory mission, but did not claim the area for the French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> On 11 June 1770, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HM_Bark_Endeavour" title="HM Bark Endeavour">HM Bark <i>Endeavour</i></a>, captained by explorer <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a>, ran <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/aground" title="wikt:aground">aground</a> on the Great Barrier Reef, sustaining considerable damage. Lightening the ship and re-floating it during an incoming tide eventually saved it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cook_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-cook-108">[108]</a></sup> One of the most famous wrecks was <a href="/wiki/HMS_Pandora_(1779)" title="HMS Pandora (1779)">HMS <i>Pandora</i></a>, which sank on 29 August 1791, killing 35 men. The <a href="/wiki/Queensland_Museum" title="Queensland Museum">Queensland Museum</a> has led archaeological digs to wreck of <i>Pandora</i> since 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Because the reef had no atolls, it was largely unstudied in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-16"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">: 7 </span></sup> During this time, some of the reef's islands were mined for deposits of <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">guano</a>, and lighthouses were built as beacons throughout the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-17"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 452">: 452 </span></sup> as in <a href="/wiki/Raine_Island" title="Raine Island">Raine Island</a>, the earliest example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> In 1922, the Great Barrier Reef Committee began carrying out much of the early research on the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HopleySmithers2007_27-18"><a href="#cite_note-HopleySmithers2007-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9">: 9 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span></h3>
