<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef_Marine_Park" title="Great Barrier Reef Marine Park">Great Barrier Reef Marine Park</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_The_Great_Barrier_Reef_Region,_World_Heritage_Area_and_Marine_Park,_2014.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2021" data-file-width="1379" decoding="async" height="454" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Map_of_The_Great_Barrier_Reef_Region%2C_World_Heritage_Area_and_Marine_Park%2C_2014.tif/lossy-page1-310px-Map_of_The_Great_Barrier_Reef_Region%2C_World_Heritage_Area_and_Marine_Park%2C_2014.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Map_of_The_Great_Barrier_Reef_Region%2C_World_Heritage_Area_and_Marine_Park%2C_2014.tif/lossy-page1-465px-Map_of_The_Great_Barrier_Reef_Region%2C_World_Heritage_Area_and_Marine_Park%2C_2014.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Map_of_The_Great_Barrier_Reef_Region%2C_World_Heritage_Area_and_Marine_Park%2C_2014.tif/lossy-page1-620px-Map_of_The_Great_Barrier_Reef_Region%2C_World_Heritage_Area_and_Marine_Park%2C_2014.tif.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_The_Great_Barrier_Reef_Region,_World_Heritage_Area_and_Marine_Park,_2014.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of The Great Barrier Reef Region, World Heritage Area and Marine Park, 2014</div></div></div>
<p>Royal Commissions disallowed oil drilling in the Great Barrier Reef, in 1975 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_Australia" title="Government of Australia">Government of Australia</a> created the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park and prohibited various activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The Great Barrier Reef Marine Park does not include the entire Great Barrier Reef Province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-map_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-map-24">[24]</a></sup> The park is managed, in partnership with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_Queensland" title="Government of Queensland">Government of Queensland</a>, through the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority to ensure that it is used in a sustainable manner. A combination of <a href="/wiki/Zoning" title="Zoning">zoning</a>, management plans, permits, education and incentives (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eco-tourism" title="Eco-tourism">eco-tourism</a> certification) are employed in the effort to conserve the reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC46_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRC46-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1999, the Australian Parliament passed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_Protection_and_Biodiversity_Conservation_Act" title="Environment Protection and Biodiversity Conservation Act">Environment Protection and Biodiversity Conservation Act</a>, which improved the operation of national environmental law by providing guidance about regional biodiversity conservation priorities. The marine bioregional planning process came from the implementation of this law. This process conserves marine biodiversity by considering the whole ecosystem a species is in and how different species interact in the marine environment.
</p><p>There are two steps to this process. The first step is to identify regional conservation priorities in the five (currently) different marine regions. The second step is to identify marine reserves (protected areas or marine parks) to be added to Australia's National Representative System of Marine Protected Areas. Like protected areas on land, marine reserves are created to protect biodiversity for generations to come. Marine reserves are identified based on criteria written in a document created by Australian and New Zealand Environment and Conservation Council called "Guidelines for establishing the national representative system of marine protected areas", also known as just "the Guidelines". These guidelines are nationally recognised and implemented at the local level based on the Australian policy for implementation outlined in the "Goals and Principles for the Establishment of the National Representative System of Marine Protected Areas in Commonwealth Waters". These policies are in place to make sure that a marine reserve is only added to the NRSMPA after careful evaluation of different data.
</p><p>The priorities for each region are created based on human and environmental threats and the Marine Bioregional Plans are drafted to address these priorities. To assess different region's priorities, three steps are taken, first, a bioregional profile is created, second, a bioregional plan is drafted, and third, the plan is finalised. After the plan is finalised, activity in different bioregions may become limited based on particular threats an activity may pose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marine_Bioregional_Planning_Process_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marine_Bioregional_Planning_Process-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001, the GBRMPA released a report about the declining water quality in the Great Barrier Reef and detailed the importance of this issue. In response to this report, in 2003, the Australian and Queensland governments launched a joint initiative to improve the quality of water entering the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The decline in the quality of water over the past 150 years (due to development) has contributed to coral bleaching, <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algal blooms</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticide_pollution" title="Pesticide pollution">pesticide pollution</a>. These forms of pollution have made the reef less resilient to climate change.
</p><p>When the plan was introduced in October 2003, it originally contained 65 actions built on previous legislation. Their immediate goal was to halt and reverse the decline in water quality entering the reef by 2013. By 2020, they hope that the quality of the water entering in the reef improves enough so that it doesn't have a detrimental impact on the health of the Great Barrier Reef. To achieve these goals they decided to reduce pollutants in the water entering the reef and to rehabilitate and conserve areas of the reef that naturally help reduce water pollutants. To achieve the objectives described above, this plan focuses on non-point sources of pollution, which cannot be traced to a single source such as a waste outlet.
</p><p>The plan specifically targets nutrients, pesticides and sediment that make their way into the reef as a result of agricultural activities. Other non-point sources of pollution that are attributed to urban areas are covered under different legislation. In 2009, the plan was updated. The updated version states that to date, none of the efforts undertaken to improve the quality of water entering the reef has been successful. The new plan attempts to address this issue by "targeting priority outcomes, integrating industry and community initiatives and incorporating new policy and regulatory frameworks (Reef Plan 5)". This updated version has improved the clarity of the previous plan and targets set by that plan, have improved accountability and further improved monitoring and assessment. The 2009 report found that 41 out of the 65 actions met their original goals, however, 18 were not progressing well according to evaluation criteria as well as 6 were rated as having unsatisfactory levels of progress.
</p><p>Some key achievements made since the plan's initial passing in 2003 were the establishment of the Reef Quality Partnership to set targets, report findings and monitor progress towards targets, improved land condition by landowners was rewarded with extended leases, Water Quality Improvement Plans were created to identify regional targets and identified management changes that needed to be made to reach those targets, Nutrient Management Zones have been created to combat <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> loss in particular areas, education programs have been started to help gather support for sustainable agriculture, changes to land management practices have taken place through the implementation of the Farm Management Systems and codes of practice, the creation of the Queensland Wetland program and other achievements were made to help improve the water quality flowing into the coral reefs.
</p><p>A taskforce of scientists was also created to assess the impact of different parts of the plan on the quality of water flowing into the coral reefs. They found that many of the goals have yet to be reached but found more evidence that states that improving the water quality of the Great Barrier Reef will improve its resilience to climate change. The Reefocus summit in 2008, which is also detailed in the report, came to similar conclusions. After this, a stakeholder working group was formed that worked between several groups as well as the Australian and Queensland governments to update reef goals and objectives. The updated version of the plan focuses on strategic priority areas and actions to achieve 2013 goals. Also quantitative targets have been made to critically assess whether targets are being met.
</p><p>Some examples of the water quality goals outlined by this plan are that by 2013, there will be a 50% reduction in nitrogen and phosphorus loads at the end of catchments and that by 2020, there will be a reduction in sediment load by 20%. The plan also outlines a number of steps that must be taken by landholders to help improve grazing, soil, nutrient, and chemical management practices. There are also a number of supporting initiatives to take place outlined in the plan to help create a framework to improve land use practices which will in turn improve water quality.
</p><p>Through these means the governments of Australia and Queensland hope to improve water quality by 2013. The 2013 outlook report and revised water quality plan will assess what needs to be done in the future to improve water quality and the livelihoods of the wildlife that resides there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WQ_protection_plan_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-WQ_protection_plan-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/170px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/255px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/340px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A blue starfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Linckia_laevigata" title="Linckia laevigata">Linckia laevigata</a></i>) resting on hard <i><a href="/wiki/Acropora" title="Acropora">Acropora</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Porites" title="Porites">Porites</a></i> corals</div></div></div>
<p>In July 2004, a new zoning plan took effect for the entire Marine Park, and has been widely acclaimed as a new global benchmark for <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">marine ecosystem</a> conservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The rezoning was based on the application of systematic conservation planning techniques, using <a href="/wiki/Marxan" title="Marxan">marxan</a> software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> While protection across the Marine Park was improved, the highly protected zones increased from 4.5% to over 33.3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> At the time, it was the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_Protected_Area" title="Marine Protected Area">Marine Protected Area</a> in the world, although in 2006, the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northwestern_Hawaiian_Islands_National_Monument" title="Northwestern Hawaiian Islands National Monument">Northwestern Hawaiian Islands National Monument</a> became the largest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_5083974_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_5083974-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, a review of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Act of 1975 recommended that there should be no further zoning plan changes until 2013, and that every five years, a <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer-reviewed</a> outlook report should be published, examining the reef's health, management, and environmental pressures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1975review_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-1975review-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In each outlook report, several assessments are required. Each assessment has a set of assessment criteria that allows for better presentation of available evidence. Each assessment is judged by these criteria and given a grade. Every outlook report follows the same judging and grading process so that information can be tracked over time. No new research is done to produce the report. Only readily available information goes into the report so little of what is known about the Reef is actually featured in each outlook report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbot_Point_coal_port_dredge_dumping_controversy">Abbot Point coal port dredge dumping controversy</span></h4>
<p>In December 2013, <a href="/wiki/Greg_Hunt" title="Greg Hunt">Greg Hunt</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_for_the_Environment_(Australia)" title="Minister for the Environment (Australia)">Australian environment minister</a>, approved a plan for dredging to create three shipping terminals as part of the construction of a coalport. According to corresponding approval documents, the process will create around 3 million cubic metres of dredged seabed that will be dumped within the Great Barrier Reef marine park area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 31 January 2014, the GBRMPA issued a dumping permit that will allow three million cubic metres of sea bed from <a href="/wiki/Abbot_Point" title="Abbot Point">Abbot Point</a>, north of Bowen, to be transported and unloaded in the waters of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park. Potential significant harms have been identified in relation to dredge spoil and the process of churning up the sea floor in the area and exposing it to air: firstly, new research shows the finer particles of dredge spoil can cloud the water and block sunlight, thereby starving sea grass and coral up to distances of 80 km away from the point of origin due to the actions of wind and currents. Furthermore, dredge spoil can literally smother reef or sea grass to death, while storms can repeatedly resuspend these particles so that the harm caused is ongoing; secondly, disturbed sea floor can release toxic substances into the surrounding environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spoil_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spoil-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>The dredge spoil from the Abbot Point port project is to be dumped 24 kilometres (15 mi) away, near Bowen in north Queensland, and the approval from the Authority will result in the production of an extra 70 million tonnes of coal annually, worth between A$1.4 billion and $2.8 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spoil_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spoil-123">[123]</a></sup> Authority chairman, Dr Russell Reichelt, stated after the confirmation of the approval:
</p>
<blockquote><p>
This approval is in line with the agency's view that port development along the Great Barrier Reef coastline should be limited to existing ports. As a deepwater port that has been in operation for nearly 30 years, Abbot Point is better placed than other ports along the Great Barrier Reef coastline to undertake expansion as the capital and maintenance dredging required will be significantly less than what would be required in other areas. It's important to note the seafloor of the approved disposal area consists of sand, silt and clay and does not contain coral reefs or seagrass beds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spoil_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-Spoil-123">[123]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The approval was provided with a corresponding set of 47 new environmental conditions that include the following:
</p>
<ul><li>A long-term water quality monitoring plan extending five years after the disposal activity is completed.</li>
<li>A heritage management plan to protect the Catalina second world war aircraft wreck in Abbot Bay.</li>
<li>The establishment of an independent dredging and disposal technical advice panel and a management response group, to include community representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spoil_123-3"><a href="#cite_note-Spoil-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marine_Parks_dredge_dumping_permit_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marine_Parks_dredge_dumping_permit-124">[124]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The Australian Federal Government announced on 13 November that there would now be a ban on the dumping of dredge spoil in the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park. The World Heritage Committee asked Environment Minister Greg Hunt to investigate alternative options to dump on land instead. The Queensland government and the Commonwealth have now accepted the alternative option and advice from The World Heritage Committee and will now commence dumping on land.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_on_the_Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Tourism on the Great Barrier Reef">Tourism on the Great Barrier Reef</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giant_clam_with_diver.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Giant_clam_with_diver.jpg/170px-Giant_clam_with_diver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Giant_clam_with_diver.jpg/255px-Giant_clam_with_diver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Giant_clam_with_diver.jpg/340px-Giant_clam_with_diver.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giant_clam_with_diver.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">scuba diver</a> looking at a <a href="/wiki/Giant_clam" title="Giant clam">giant clam</a> on the Great Barrier Reef</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amazing_Great_Barrier_Reef_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Amazing_Great_Barrier_Reef_1.jpg/220px-Amazing_Great_Barrier_Reef_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Amazing_Great_Barrier_Reef_1.jpg/330px-Amazing_Great_Barrier_Reef_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Amazing_Great_Barrier_Reef_1.jpg/440px-Amazing_Great_Barrier_Reef_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amazing_Great_Barrier_Reef_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Helicopter view of the reef and boats</div></div></div>
<p>Due to its vast <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, warm clear waters and accessibility from the tourist boats called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Live_aboard" title="Live aboard">live aboards</a>", the reef is a very popular destination, especially for <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">scuba divers</a>. Tourism on the Great Barrier Reef is concentrated in Cairns and also The Whitsundays due to their accessibility. These areas make up 7%–8% of the park's area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC46_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRC46-56">[56]</a></sup> The Whitsundays and <a href="/wiki/Cairns" title="Cairns">Cairns</a> have their own Plans of Management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005Brochure_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-2005Brochure-126">[126]</a></sup> Many cities along the Queensland coast offer daily boat trips. Several continental and coral cay islands are now <a href="/wiki/Resort" title="Resort">resorts</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Green_Island_(Queensland)" title="Green Island (Queensland)">Green Island</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lady_Elliot_Island" title="Lady Elliot Island">Lady Elliot Island</a>. As of 1996, 27 islands on the Great Barrier Reef supported resorts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC46_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-CRC46-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1996, most of the tourism in the region was domestically generated and the most popular visiting times were during the Australian winter. At this time, it was estimated that tourists to the Great Barrier Reef contributed <a href="/wiki/Australian_dollar" title="Australian dollar">A$</a>776 million per annum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> As the largest commercial activity in the region, it was estimated in 2003 that tourism generated over A$4 billion annually,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> and the 2005 estimate increased to A$5.1 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005Brochure_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-2005Brochure-126">[126]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Deloitte" title="Deloitte">Deloitte</a> report published by the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority in March 2013 states that the Reef's 2,000 kilometres of coastline attracts tourism worth A$6.4 billion annually and employs more than 64,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Approximately two million people visit the Great Barrier Reef each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Although most of these visits are managed in partnership with the marine Tourism industry, there is a concern among the general public that tourism is harmful to the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC46_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-CRC46-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>A variety of boat tours and cruises are offered, from single day trips, to longer voyages. Boat sizes range from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinghies" title="Dinghies">dinghies</a> to <a href="/wiki/Superyacht" title="Superyacht">superyachts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glass_bottom_boat" title="Glass bottom boat">Glass-bottomed boats</a> and underwater <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">observatories</a> are also popular, as are <a href="/wiki/Helicopter" title="Helicopter">helicopter</a> flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> By far, the most popular tourist activities on the Great Barrier Reef are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snorkelling" title="Snorkelling">snorkelling</a> and diving, for which pontoons are often used, and the area is often enclosed by nets. The outer part of the Great Barrier Reef is favoured for such activities, due to water quality.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Management of tourism in the Great Barrier Reef is geared towards making tourism <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological_sustainability" title="Ecological sustainability">ecologically sustainable</a>. A daily fee is levied that goes towards research of the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005Brochure_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-2005Brochure-126">[126]</a></sup> This fee ends up being 20% of the GBRMPA's income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
Policies on <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ships</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bareboat_charter" title="Bareboat charter">bareboat charters</a>, and anchorages limit the traffic on the Great Barrier Reef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005Brochure_126-3"><a href="#cite_note-2005Brochure-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>The problems that surround ecotourism in the Great Barrier Reef revolve around permanent tourism platforms. Platforms are large, ship-like vessels that act as a base for tourists while scuba diving and snorkelling in the Great Barrier Reef. Seabirds will land on the platforms and defecate which will eventually be washed into the sea. The feces carry nitrogen, phosphorus and often <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, which cause <a href="/wiki/Aspergillosis" title="Aspergillosis">aspergillosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellow-band_disease" title="Yellow-band disease">yellow-band disease</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_band_disease" title="Black band disease">black band disease</a>. Areas without tourism platforms have 14 out of 9,468 (1.1%) diseased corals versus areas with tourism platforms that have 172 out of 7,043 (12%) diseased corals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Tourism is a major economic activity for the region. Thus, while non-permanent platforms could be possible in some areas, overall, permanent platforms are likely a necessity. Solutions have been suggested to siphon bird waste into gutters connecting to tanks helping lower runoff that causes coral disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority has also placed many permanent anchorage points around the general use areas. These act to reduce damage to the reef due to anchoring destroying soft coral, chipping hard coral, and disturbing sediment as it is dragged across the bottom. Tourism operators also must comply with speed limits when travelling to or from tourist destinations, to prevent excessive wake from the boats disturbing the reef ecosystem.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fishing">Fishing</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry" title="Fishing industry">fishing industry</a> in the Great Barrier Reef, controlled by the Queensland Government, is worth <a href="/wiki/Australian_dollar" title="Australian dollar">A$</a>1 billion annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economics_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-economics-15">[15]</a></sup> It employs approximately 2000 people, and fishing in the Great Barrier Reef is pursued commercially, for recreation, and as a traditional means for feeding one's family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-traditionalfishing_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-traditionalfishing-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dugong_hunting">Dugong hunting</span></h3>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Native_Title_Act_1993" title="Native Title Act 1993">Native Title Act 1993</a>, native title holders <a href="/wiki/Dugong_hunting_in_Australia" title="Dugong hunting in Australia">retain the right to legally hunt dugongs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_turtle" title="Green turtle">green turtles</a> for "personal, domestic or non-commercial communal needs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (October 2016)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Four traditional owners groups agreed to cease the hunting of <a href="/wiki/Dugong" title="Dugong">dugongs</a> in the area in 2011 due to their declining numbers, partially accelerated by seagrass damage from <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Yasi" title="Cyclone Yasi">Cyclone Yasi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
